        <meta property="og:url" content="https://pubs.acs.org/doi/full/10.1021/acsomega.2c06429"><meta property="og:title" content="Crab Shell-Derived SnS2/C and FeS2/C Carbon Composites as Anodes for High-Performance Sodium-Ion Batteries"><meta property="og:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acsomega.2c06429/asset/images/acsomega.2c06429.social.jpeg_v03"><meta property="og:description" content="The demand for energy storage devices has increased significantly, and the sustainable development of lithium-ion batteries is limited by scarce lithium resources. Therefore, alternative sodium-ion batteries which are rich in resource may become more competitive in the future market. In this work, we synthesized low-cost SnS2/C and FeS2/C anode materials of sodium-ion batteries which used waste crab shells as biomass carbon precursor. The SnS2 nanosheet and FeS2 nanosphere structures are deposited on the crab shell-derived carbon through simple hydrothermal reaction. Due to the coexistence of transition metal dichalcogenides (TMDs) and crab-derived biomass carbon, the anode material has excellent cycle stability and rate performance. SnS2/C and FeS2/C deliver capacities of 535.4 and 479 mA h g–1 at the current density of 0.1 A g–1, respectively. This study explored an effective and economical strategy to use biomass and TMDs to construct high-performance sodium-ion batteries."><meta property="og:type" content="Article"><meta property="og:site_name" content="ACS Publications"><meta name="twitter:card" content="summary_large_image"><meta name="twitter:site" content="@ACSPublications"><meta name="twitter:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acsomega.2c06429/asset/images/acsomega.2c06429.social.jpeg_v03">

<!-- loading Javascript for using the persistence Service -->
    <script src="/wro/nwyw~thiss-ds.js" async="" nonce="90f6ef5f5d47775c-SJC"></script>

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="90f6ef5f5d47775c-SJC">

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            timeout: 5000,

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
            },
            retrieveEntity: function (ms) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                if (ms == undefined || ms === '') {
                    ms = this.timeout;
                }

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res.data);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('failed to retrieve entity from local storage: ' + err);
                        });
                    // Set up the timeout
                    setTimeout(function () {
                        reject('Promise timed out ' + ms + ' ms');
                    }, ms);
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage due to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {
                            doi: doi,
                            entityid: entityId,
                            entityTitle: entityTitle,
                            eventGroupKey: '3ad1a813-988c-4df7-a5bc-eda84166c2d2'
                        }

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    }).fail(function (status) {
                        return reject(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (i = 0; i < allSsoInstitutions.length; i++) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        if(allSsoInstitutions.length === 0 && true){
                            self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, null, null)
                                .then(function () {
                                    console.log('No Entity found');
                                }, function (err) {
                                    console.log('failed to get the none entity status' ,err);
                                });
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            }).catch(function (err) {
                                reject(err);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();

                    if ($('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').length > 0) {
                        let institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox = $('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').get(0);
                        if (!$(institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox).is(":checked")) {
                            return;
                        }
                    }

                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

        <script async="" type="text/javascript" src="https://www.googletagservices.com/tag/js/gpt.js" rel="preconnect" nonce="90f6ef5f5d47775c-SJC"></script>

<script type="text/javascript" nonce="90f6ef5f5d47775c-SJC">
var dfpReady = false;
$(document).ready(function() {
    // var journalTitle = 'omega';
    var taxonomyKeywords = $('.article_header-taxonomy');
    var articleKeyword = $('meta[name="keywords"]');
    var articleTitle = $('meta[name="dc.Title"]');
    var articleDescription = $('meta[name="dc.Description"]');
    var abstractKeyword = $('p.articleBody_abstractText:contains("Keywords: ")');
    var searchTerms = [];

    if (taxonomyKeywords.length > 0) {
        $(taxonomyKeywords).children(".rlist--inline").children("li").each(function() {
            searchTerms.push($(this).text());
        });
    }
    else if (articleKeyword.length > 0) { 
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleKeyword.prop('content').split(', '));
    }
    else if (abstractKeyword.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(abstractKeyword.text().split("Keywords: ")[1].split('; '));
    }
    else if (articleDescription.length > 0) {
        $(articleDescription).each(function() {
            articleDescriptionKeywords = $(this).prop('content');
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/.,/g,"");
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/\s{2,}/g," ");
            searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleDescriptionKeywords.split(' '));
        });
    }

    if (articleTitle.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleTitle.prop('content').split(' '));
    }

    googletag.cmd.push(function() {
        var mappingLeader = googletag.sizeMapping().
        addSize([750, 200], [728, 90]). 
        addSize([0, 0], [320, 50]).
        build();
        //leaderboard
        googletag.defineSlot('/8868/journal/omega/article', [728, 90], 'dfp-journal-article-leaderboard').defineSizeMapping(mappingLeader).setTargeting('location', 'leaderboard1').setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //recommend
        googletag.defineSlot("/8868/journal/omega/article", [1, 1], "dfp-journal-article-recommend").setTargeting("ntvPlacement", ["1100167"]).setTargeting("location", "recommend").setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //googletag.pubads().enableSingleRequest();
        googletag.pubads().collapseEmptyDivs();
        googletag.pubads().setTargeting('inst',dartInstitutionType).setTargeting('memb',dartMember);
        googletag.pubads().addEventListener("slotRenderEnded", function(event) {
            console.log(event.slot.getSlotElementId() + " isEmpty: " + event.isEmpty + " | creative id: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            if((event.isEmpty === false) && (event.slot.getSlotElementId() != "dfp-journal-article-recommend")) {
                $("#" + event.slot.getSlotElementId()).parents(".advertisement").slideDown();
                console.log("ad display: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            }
            // else {
            //     console.log("no ad found");
            // }
        });
        googletag.enableServices();
        dfpReady = true;
    });
});
</script>

class="icon-check-circle"></i><span><strong>Cite This:</strong></span></a><span class="article_header-cite-this figure-viewer-cite-this"><span class="cit-fg-title"><i>ACS Omega</i></span><span class="cit-fg-year"> 2023</span><span class="cit-fg-volume">, 8</span><span class="cit-fg-issue">, 10</span><span class="cit-fg-pageRange">, <script nonce="90f6ef5f5d47775c-SJC">
$(function() {
    var serialCode = "acsodf";
    var books = ["reagents", "symposium", "advances", "medichemreviews", "styleguide"]
    if(books.includes(serialCode) === false) {
        // default click action
        $("[data-id]").on("mousedown", function(e) {
            var currentClickID = $(this).attr("data-id");

            if(currentClickID.includes("_hover") === false) {
                if(currentClickID.match("^article_") && !currentClickID.match("^article_abstract_CC")) {   
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Crab Shell-Derived SnS&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;/C and FeS&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;/C Carbon Composites as Anodes for High-Performance Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsomega.2c06429", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                }
            }
        });
        
        // Access Management icons hover
        var articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        var articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent === false) {
                articleAccessManagementIconHover = true;
                var articleAccessManagementHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleAccessManagementIconHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Crab Shell-Derived SnS&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;/C and FeS&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;/C Carbon Composites as Anodes for High-Performance Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsomega.2c06429", "Article_Element": articleAccessManagementHover });
    
                        articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        });
    
        // articleRightsPermissionsLink hover
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent === false) {
                articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = true;
                var articleRightsHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Crab Shell-Derived SnS&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;/C and FeS&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;/C Carbon Composites as Anodes for High-Performance Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsomega.2c06429", "Article_Element": articleRightsHover });
    
                        articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        });
    
        // Author hover
        var articleAuthorHover = false
        var articleAuthorHoverEvent = false
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if((articleAuthorHoverEvent === false) && ($(this).parent().hasClass("view-affs") === false)) {
                articleAuthorHover = true;
                var authorInfoHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAuthorHover === true) && (articleAuthorHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Crab Shell-Derived SnS&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;/C and FeS&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;/C Carbon Composites as Anodes for High-Performance Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsomega.2c06429", "Article_Element": authorInfoHover });
    
                        articleAuthorHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAuthorHover = false;
        });
    
        // CC License hover
        var articleAbstractCCHover = false
        var articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = false
        var articleDrawerCCHover = false
        var articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = false
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if($(this).attr("data-id")) {
                if(($(this).attr("data-id") === "article_drawer_CC-License") && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleDrawerCCHover = true;
                    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleDrawerCCHover === true) && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Crab Shell-Derived SnS&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;/C and FeS&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;/C Carbon Composites as Anodes for High-Performance Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsomega.2c06429", "Article_Element": "article_drawer_CC-License_hover" });
    
                            articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
                else if(($(this).attr("data-id").match("^article_abstract_CC")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleAbstractCCHover = true;
    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Crab Shell-Derived SnS&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;/C and FeS&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;/C Carbon Composites as Anodes for High-Performance Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsomega.2c06429", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                            articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
            }
            else if(($(this).hasClass("article__cc-license__link")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = true;
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Crab Shell-Derived SnS&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;/C and FeS&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;/C Carbon Composites as Anodes for High-Performance Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsomega.2c06429", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                        articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            if(articleAbstractCCHover === true) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = false;
            }
            if(articleDrawerCCHover = true) {
                articleDrawerCCHover = false;
            }
        });
        
        // show recommeneded click delay
        var intervalCount = 0;
        var showRecommendedInterval = setInterval(function() {
            intervalCount += 100;
            if($(".show-recommended__wrapper .show-recommended").length > 0) {
                clearInterval(showRecommendedInterval);
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowMore']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Crab Shell-Derived SnS&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;/C and FeS&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;/C Carbon Composites as Anodes for High-Performance Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsomega.2c06429", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowLess']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Crab Shell-Derived SnS&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;/C and FeS&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;/C Carbon Composites as Anodes for High-Performance Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsomega.2c06429", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
            }
        }, 100);
    }
});
</script>

                                            </p></div></div></div></div></div></div></div></div></div><div class="article_header-metrics-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="article_header-metrics-dropzone"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div></div></div></div><div class="article_abstract"><div class="article_abstract-title" id="Abstract" tabindex="0"><h2>Abstract</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div><div class="article_abstract-content hlFld-Abstract" id="abstractBox" property="description"><figure id="_i1" data-index="0" class="article__inlineFigure article_abstract-img"><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img class="inline-fig internalNav" src="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.2c06429/asset/images/medium/ao2c06429_0012.gif" alt="" id="tgr1" property="image" style="width:405px; height:275px"></button><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.2c06429/asset/images/large/ao2c06429_0012.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsomega.2c06429&amp;id=_i1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p class="articleBody_abstractText">The demand for energy storage devices has increased significantly, and the sustainable development of lithium-ion batteries is limited by scarce lithium resources. Therefore, alternative sodium-ion batteries which are rich in resource may become more competitive in the future market. In this work, we synthesized low-cost SnS<sub>2</sub>/C and FeS<sub>2</sub>/C anode materials of sodium-ion batteries which used waste crab shells as biomass carbon precursor. The SnS<sub>2</sub> nanosheet and FeS<sub>2</sub> nanosphere structures are deposited on the crab shell-derived carbon through simple hydrothermal reaction. Due to the coexistence of transition metal dichalcogenides (TMDs) and crab-derived biomass carbon, the anode material has excellent cycle stability and rate performance. SnS<sub>2</sub>/C and FeS<sub>2</sub>/C deliver capacities of 535.4 and 479 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at the current density of 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively. This study explored an effective and economical strategy to use biomass and TMDs to construct high-performance sodium-ion batteries.</p></div><div class="article__cc-license"><div class="article__cc-license__body"><p class="article__cc-license__para">This publication is licensed under</p><div class="article__cc-license__inter"><div class="article__cc-license--hoverable"><a href="https://creativecommons.org/licenses/by-nc-nd/4.0/" aria-describedby="tooltip-cc-text-abstract" class="link--bold" data-id="article_abstract_CC-License">CC-BY-NC-ND 4.0
                                    <span class="arrow"></span></a>
                                .

article__tags--subjects"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Subjects</h3><div tabindex="0" class="article__tags__tooltip"><div aria-describedby="tooltip-subjects" class="article__tags__icon"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are subjects</span></div><p id="tooltip-subjects" role="tooltip" class="article__tags__diff">Article subjects are automatically applied from the ACS Subject Taxonomy and describe the scientific concepts and themes of the article.</p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291517&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Batteries" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Batteries" class="article__tags__link">Batteries</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291337&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Carbon" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Carbon" class="article__tags__link">Carbon</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291331&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Composites" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Composites" class="article__tags__link">Composites</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291338&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Electrical properties" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Electrical%20properties" class="article__tags__link">Electrical properties</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291371&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Electrodes" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Electrodes" class="article__tags__link">Electrodes</a></li></ul></div></div></div></div></div><div class="article_content"><div class="article_content-table hlFld-FullText"><div class="article_content-row"><div class="article_content-left" property="articleBody" style="width: 384px;"><div id="sec1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i2">Introduction</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">Owing to its high energy density and long cycle life, lithium-ion batteries have been widely used in the field of portable energy storage devices. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref1 ref2 ref3 ref4 ref5'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1 ref2 ref3 ref4 ref5" aria-label="reference 1 to 5">(1−5)</a> However, their sustainable development is limited by scarce lithium resources. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref6'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref6" aria-label="reference 6">(6)</a> Therefore, alternative devices may become more competitive in the future market, especially for large energy storage systems such as electric vehicles and fixed grid storage. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref7 ref8 ref9 ref10 ref11 ref12 ref13'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref7 ref8 ref9 ref10 ref11 ref12 ref13" aria-label="reference 7 to 13">(7−13)</a> Compared with lithium, sodium is rich in resource and low in cost, and its redox potential is relatively close to that of lithium, so sodium-ion battery has high expectations on the application of large-scale energy storage systems. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref14 ref15 ref16 ref17 ref18'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref14 ref15 ref16 ref17 ref18" aria-label="reference 14 to 18">(14−18)</a> Currently, graphene is the most widely used anode material for lithium batteries. However, due to the large radius of sodium ions (0.95 Å Na <sup>+</sup> is larger than 0.60 Å Li <sup>+</sup>) that makes insertion difficult, graphite almost has no sodium storage activity. At present, widely studied sodium-ion battery anode materials include hard carbon, metals, metal oxides/sulfides, alloys, organic compounds, and so forth. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref19 ref20 ref21 ref22'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref19 ref20 ref21 ref22" aria-label="reference 19 to 22">(19−22)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">Among TMDs, Sn-based transition-metal sulfides have been extensively studied because of their high theoretical capacity (1136 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>). The high capacity of Sn-based transition-metal sulfide is attributed to the combined effect of the conversion reaction and the alloying reaction. However, the poor conductivity of the Sn-based sulfide anode materials limits their electrochemical performance. The Fe-based transition-metal sulfide has a high theoretical capacity of 894 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> and has been extensively studied due to its low cost and non-toxic characteristics. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref9 ref23 ref24 ref25 ref26'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref9 ref23 ref24 ref25 ref26" aria-label="reference 9 and 23 to 26">(9,23−26)</a> Although SnS<sub>2</sub> and FeS<sub>2</sub> have high theoretical capacities, they are far from expected in actual applications. During alloying reactions, there are significant volume expansion, crushing, and mechanical deformation, which result in poor cycle stability and capacity. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref27'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref27" aria-label="reference 27">(27)</a> The active materials are easy to coarsen, which hugely limits the progress of the reverse reaction and interferes with the energy density of the sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) adversely. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref28 ref29 ref30 ref31 ref32 ref33 ref34'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref28 ref29 ref30 ref31 ref32 ref33 ref34" aria-label="reference 28 to 34">(28−34)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">Generally, there are three common ways to improve sodium-ion storage for sodium-ion batteries: (1) composite TMDs with carbon materials. The excellent conductivity of carbon materials can not only promote the transmission of Na<sup>+</sup> but also provide a buffer layer for volume expansion during the conversion process and consequently improve cycle stability and rate performance. (2) Regulating the morphology of transition-metal sulfides. A good structure can shorten the diffusion distance of sodium ions, providing enough space for volume expansion. (3) Constructing bimetallic sulfide. Compared with monometallic sulfide, bimetallic sulfide contains more active sites, and the constructed heterojunction can further improve the electrical conductivity and enhance the electrochemical performance. (4) Selection of suitable electrolytes. A suitable electrolyte which affects the formation of the solid electrolyte interface (SEI) is an important factor in determining the battery performance. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref35'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref35" aria-label="reference 35">(35)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">Recently, biomass-derived hard carbon materials have attracted widespread attention as electrode materials for SIBs because of the superior performance, eco-friendliness, abundance, and renewability. However, the low theoretical capacity limits its application. Therefore, a strategy that combines TMDs and biomass-based hard carbon can not only improve cycle stability but also have a higher capacity and a lower cost, thus providing a better solution for industrialization. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref33 ref36 ref37 ref38'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref33 ref36 ref37 ref38" aria-label="reference 33 and 36 to 38">(33,36−38)</a></div><div class="NLM_p last">Herein, we synthesized SnS<sub>2</sub>/C and FeS<sub>2</sub>/C composites which used waste crab shell as hard carbon biomass precursor. SnS<sub>2</sub> nanosheet and FeS<sub>2</sub> nanosphere structures are deposited on the crab shell-derived carbon through a simple hydrothermal reaction. Due to the coexistence of metal sulfides and crab carbon with a high surface area, the composite material has excellent cycle performance and rate capability and has long-term stability. When used as the anode of sodium-ion batteries, SnS<sub>2</sub>/C and FeS<sub>2</sub>/C deliver the capacities of 416.5 and 393.8 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at the current density of 0.5 mA g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively, after 200 cycles. This study provides an economical strategy to use biomass and TMDs to construct high-performance sodium-ion batteries.</div></div><div id="sec2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i3">Experiment Section</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec2_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i4" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i4"> Synthesis of Crab Carbon</h3><div class="NLM_p">Crab carbon was prepared by one-step carbonization of crab shell (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>). The pre-treated crab shell was carbonized at 700 °C for 2 h under the atmosphere of Ar gas. After purification in 3 M HCl solution, washing by deionized water, and drying at 120 °C overnight in a vacuum oven, the crab carbon was obtained.</div><figure id="fig1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.2c06429/asset/images/medium/ao2c06429_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.2c06429/asset/images/large/ao2c06429_0002.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. Schematic illustration of the fabrication process of the crab carbon and SnS<sub>2</sub>/C and FeS<sub>2</sub>/C composites.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.2c06429/asset/images/large/ao2c06429_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsomega.2c06429&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure></div><div id="sec2_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i5" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i5"> Synthesis of SnS<sub>2</sub>/C and FeS<sub>2</sub>/C Composite Materials</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The SnS<sub>2</sub>/C and FeS<sub>2</sub>/C composites were prepared by a facile hydrothermal method (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>). First, 30 mL of deionized water and 20 mL of ethanol were mixed with stirring for 2 h. 0.7 g of SnCl<sub>4</sub>·5H<sub>2</sub>O was added dropwise to the solution to form Sn<sup>4+</sup> solution. Then 0.45 g of CH<sub>3</sub>CSNH<sub>2</sub>, 2 mL of acetic acid (HAc), and 0.8 g of the as-prepared crab carbon were added to the Sn<sup>4+</sup> solution. The above solution was transferred to a Teflon-lined autoclave and kept at 160 °C for 16 h. Finally, the product composite was washed twice with water and ethanol and then freeze-dried for 24 h. The synthesized method of FeS<sub>2</sub>/C is the same as that of SnS<sub>2</sub>/C using 0.54 g of FeCl<sub>3</sub>·5H<sub>2</sub>O as a reagent.</div></div></div><div id="sec3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i6">Results and Discussion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">We first used X-ray diffraction (XRD) patterns to analyze the composition and purity of the synthesized crab carbon and SnS<sub>2</sub>/C and FeS<sub>2</sub>/C composites (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>). The strong and broad diffraction peaks in the crab carbon sample indicate that it has amorphous graphitic phase. SnS<sub>2</sub>/C has broad diffraction peaks and the diffraction peaks of (001), (100), (101), and (110) lattice planes, which are in good agreement with SnS<sub>2</sub> (JCPDS no. 23-0677). The pattern of FeS<sub>2</sub>/C has the broad diffraction peaks which belong to amorphous carbon, and the diffraction peaks of (111), (200), (210), (211), (220), and (311) are attributed to FeS<sub>2</sub> (JCPDS no. 23-0677). This shows that our synthesized product does not contain other impurities, and the target samples are obtained.</div><figure id="fig2" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.2c06429/asset/images/medium/ao2c06429_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.2c06429/asset/images/large/ao2c06429_0003.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. XRD pattern of crab carbon and SnS<sub>2</sub>/C and FeS<sub>2</sub>/C composites.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.2c06429/asset/images/large/ao2c06429_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsomega.2c06429&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The scanning electron microscopy (SEM) images show that the morphology of crab carbon is a porous hierarchical layered structure (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>a,b). After 700 °C carbonization, the chitin protein nanofibers in crab shell transformed into carbon skeleton. Crab carbon exhibits a typical 3D interconnected honeycomb hierarchical structure and contains a lot of pores, which greatly increases the specific surface area. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref39 ref40 ref41 ref42 ref43'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref39 ref40 ref41 ref42 ref43" aria-label="reference 39 to 43">(39−43)</a> Compared with the flat porous structure of crab carbon, the SnS<sub>2</sub>/C sample has a superimposed multilayer porous structure (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>c,d), which is beneficial for the sodiation and desodiation of sodium ions. FeS<sub>2</sub>/C is constituted by FeS<sub>2</sub> nanospheres and porous crab carbon (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>e). Moreover, FeS<sub>2</sub> nanospheres are uniformly distributed on the surface of crab carbon (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>f).</div><figure id="fig3" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.2c06429/asset/images/medium/ao2c06429_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.2c06429/asset/images/large/ao2c06429_0004.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. Morphology and structural characterization of SEM images of (a,b) crab carbon, (c,d) SnS<sub>2</sub>/C, and (e,f) FeS<sub>2</sub>/C.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.2c06429/asset/images/large/ao2c06429_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsomega.2c06429&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">Transmission electron microscopy (TEM) was used to analyze the future details of the SnS<sub>2</sub>/C and FeS<sub>2</sub>/C composites. It shows that SnS<sub>2</sub>/C composite has many fibers with a pore structure, which is crab carbon precursor, and the SnS<sub>2</sub> nanosheets which are evenly distributed on the surface of crab carbon (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>a,b). It shows that on a smaller scale, the SnS<sub>2</sub> layered structure is composed of nanosheets. This nanofiber pore and nanosheet hybrid structure not only shorten the sodium-ion transmission distance but also reduce electrical resistance and provide more buffer layers for volume expansion. Energy-dispersive X-ray spectroscopy (EDX) element mapping displays that the elements of S, Sn, and C are evenly distributed on the surface of SnS<sub>2</sub>/C composite (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>c–f).</div><figure id="fig4" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.2c06429/asset/images/medium/ao2c06429_0005.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.2c06429/asset/images/large/ao2c06429_0005.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. Morphology and structural characterization: (a,b) TEM images and (c–f) elemental mapping images of SnS<sub>2</sub>/C composite.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.2c06429/asset/images/large/ao2c06429_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsomega.2c06429&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">It shows that FeS<sub>2</sub>/C composite is constituted of nanofibers (crab carbon) and nanospheres (FeS<sub>2</sub>) (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>a,b). EDX element mapping displays that the elements of S, Fe, and C are distributed on the surface of SnS<sub>2</sub>/C composite (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>c–f). This proves that the structure and morphology of crab do not change when it compounds with Sn<sub>2</sub>S and Fe<sub>2</sub>S.</div><figure id="fig5" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.2c06429/asset/images/medium/ao2c06429_0006.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.2c06429/asset/images/large/ao2c06429_0006.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. Morphology and structural characterization: (a,b) TEM images and (c–f) elemental mapping images of FeS<sub>2</sub>/C composite.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.2c06429/asset/images/large/ao2c06429_0006.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsomega.2c06429&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">From the Raman spectrums (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>a), two peaks appear at 1340 and 1575 cm<sup>–1</sup>, which are related to disordered carbon (D band) and graphitic carbon (G band), respectively. The ratio of <i>I</i><sub>G</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>D</sub> represents the degree of graphitization of the sample. The analysis shows that the <i>I</i><sub>G</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>D</sub> of the three samples are very close. It also indicates that crab carbon does not change much after compounding sulfides. Brunauer–Emmett–Teller (BET) method displays that crab carbon (272 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>–1</sup>) has a larger surface area than SnS<sub>2</sub>/C (186 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>–1</sup>) and FeS<sub>2</sub> (102 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>–1</sup>) (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>b). The large specific surface area of crab carbon can provide more ion transport channels, which is conducive to the fast transport of sodium ions. Compared with the nanosphere structure of FeS<sub>2</sub>, the nanosheet structure of SnS<sub>2</sub> provides a larger specific surface area.</div><figure id="fig6" data-index="6" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 6</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.2c06429/asset/images/medium/ao2c06429_0007.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.2c06429/asset/images/large/ao2c06429_0007.jpeg" id="gr6" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 6. (a) Raman spectra and (b) nitrogen adsorption–desorption isotherms of crab carbon, SnS<sub>2</sub>/C, and FeS<sub>2</sub>/C samples.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.2c06429/asset/images/large/ao2c06429_0007.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsomega.2c06429&amp;id=fig6"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy can further analyze the element composition and surface electronic state of the sample. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 7</a>a, it clearly indicates that FeS<sub>2</sub>/C contains the elements of S, O, C, and Fe. The presence of O can be attributed to the oxidation of the sample in the air. For Fe 2p spectrum (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 7</a>c), the peaks at 719.9 eV and 707 eV can be attributed to the presence of Fe<sup>2+</sup> and 711.1 eV corresponds to Fe<sup>3+</sup>. For the S 2p spectrum (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 7</a>e), it can be divided into two peaks at 162.8 eV (2P<sub>1/2</sub>) and 161.7 eV (2P<sub>3/2</sub>), which correspond to S<sup>2–</sup> (FeS<sub>2</sub>). For SnS<sub>2</sub>/C sample (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 7</a>b), it contains the elements of C, Sn, and S. The Sn 3d spectrum (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 7</a>d) contains the peaks at 486.6 and 495.1 eV which can be attributed to Sn 3d<sub>5/2</sub>, and Sn 3d<sub>3/2</sub>, respectively. For the S 2p spectrum (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 7</a>f), it can also be divided into two peaks at 163.6 eV (2P<sub>1/2</sub>) and 162.4 eV (2P<sub>3/2</sub>) which correspond to S<sup>2–</sup> (SnS<sub>2</sub>).</div><figure id="fig7" data-index="7" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 7</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.2c06429/asset/images/medium/ao2c06429_0008.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.2c06429/asset/images/large/ao2c06429_0008.jpeg" id="gr7" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 7. Spectroscopic analysis of FeS<sub>2</sub>/C and SnS<sub>2</sub>/C. The XPS spectra of (a) full elements, (c) Fe 2p, and (e) S 2p of FeS<sub>2</sub>/C sample. The XPS spectra of (b) full elements, (d) Sn 3d, and (f) S 2p of SnS<sub>2</sub>/C sample.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.2c06429/asset/images/large/ao2c06429_0008.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsomega.2c06429&amp;id=fig7"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p"><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 8</a>a,b shows the charge–discharge curves of the SnS<sub>2</sub>/C and FeS<sub>2</sub>/C composites during the initial three cycles at a current density of 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup>. For SnS<sub>2</sub>/C anode material, the specific capacities of the first cycle of discharge and charge are 713.4 and 550.1 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively, corresponding to 77% of initial Coulombic efficiency. The specific capacities of the first cycle of FeS<sub>2</sub>/C composite discharge and charge are 903.2 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> and 603.9 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively, corresponding to 66.8% of initial Coulombic efficiency. To further demonstrate the superiority of the SnS<sub>2</sub>/C and FeS<sub>2</sub>/C anode materials, we have tested the electrochemical properties of different samples and compare them in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 8</a>c,d. The crab carbon, SnS<sub>2</sub>/C, and FeS<sub>2</sub>/C deliver capacities of 262.5, 535.4, and 479 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at the current density of 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup> after 50 cycles, respectively. After compounding with sulfide, the composite still exhibits good stability and the specific capacity has been significantly improved. When the current density increased from 0.1 to 0.5 A g<sup>–1</sup>, crab carbon, SnS<sub>2</sub>/C, and FeS<sub>2</sub>/C deliver the capacities of 192.3, 416.5, and 393.8 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively, after 200 cycles (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 8</a>d). The capacity retention rate of SnS<sub>2</sub>/C is 60%, and that of FeS<sub>2</sub>/C is 64%. This means that the synthesized composite material has good stability under a large current density and has the potential for fast charging applications. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 8</a>e,f shows the rate performance recover ability of SnS<sub>2</sub>/C and FeS<sub>2</sub>/C electrode materials. For SnS<sub>2</sub>/C anode, the specific capacities are 572.8, 465.2, 329.3, 243.4, and 167.6 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at the currently densities of 0.1, 0.2, 0.5, 1, and 2 A g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively. When the current density returns to 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup>, the specific capacity returns to 552 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>, indicating that the composite material has excellent rate performance. The result shows that the specific capacity of the battery under the rate performance is not as good as the direct current test. For FeS<sub>2</sub>/C electrode material, the specific capacities are 567.5, 460.2, 339.5, 220.5, and 220.5 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at the current densities of 0.1, 0.2, 0.5, 1, and 2 A g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively. When the current density returns to 0.1A g<sup>–1</sup>, the specific capacity returns to 482.2 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>. It proves that the FeS<sub>2</sub>/C composite anode also has good recover capacity.</div><figure id="fig8" data-index="8" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 8</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.2c06429/asset/images/medium/ao2c06429_0009.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.2c06429/asset/images/large/ao2c06429_0009.jpeg" id="gr8" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 8. Typical discharge/charge curves of (a) SnS<sub>2</sub>/C and (b) FeS<sub>2</sub>/C composites at the current density of 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup>. The cycling performance of crab carbon, SnS<sub>2</sub>/C, and FeS<sub>2</sub>/C at the current densities of (c) 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup> and (d) 0.5 A g<sup>–1</sup> between 0.005 and 3.0 V. Rate capability of charge capacity of (e) SnS<sub>2</sub>/C and (f) FeS<sub>2</sub>/C at different current densities.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.2c06429/asset/images/large/ao2c06429_0009.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsomega.2c06429&amp;id=fig8"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The cyclic voltammetry (CV) test was used to analyze the reactions of electrode materials during cycling, which was scanned at 0.1 mV s<sup>–1</sup> between 0.01 and 3.0 V, as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig9" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 9</a>. For SnS<sub>2</sub>/C, the cathodic scan of the first cycle, a strong peak around 1.0 V is due to the conversion reaction [SnS<sub>2</sub> +Na<sup>+</sup> → Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>SnS<sub>2</sub> (<i>x</i> &lt; 2)] and the formation of SEI. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref44'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref44" aria-label="reference 44">(44)</a> A weak peak around 0.2 V is attributed to the conversion reaction (Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>SnS<sub>2</sub> → Sn<sup>0</sup> + Na<sub>2</sub>S). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref45'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref45" aria-label="reference 45">(45)</a> For the anodic scan of the other cycles, the peak around 1.6 V is due to the form of Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>SnS<sub>2</sub> and the SEI layers. The peak around 2.3 V can be ascribed to the reaction of Sn<sup>0</sup>/Na<sub>2</sub>S and irreversible SEI layer (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig9" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 9</a>a). For FeS<sub>2</sub>, the resulting redox reaction is similar to that of SnS<sub>2</sub>, the reduction peak is at 1.1 V, and oxidation peaks are around 1.4 and 2.2 V (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig9" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 9</a>b).</div><figure id="fig9" data-index="9" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 9</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.2c06429/asset/images/medium/ao2c06429_0010.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.2c06429/asset/images/large/ao2c06429_0010.jpeg" id="gr9" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 9. Cyclic voltammograms of (a) SnS<sub>2</sub>/C and (b) FeS<sub>2</sub>/C samples at a scan rate of 0.1 mV s<sup>–1</sup> between 0.01 and 3 V.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.2c06429/asset/images/large/ao2c06429_0010.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsomega.2c06429&amp;id=fig9"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">We used electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) to analyze the conductivity of the electrode materials in the sodium-ion batteries. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig10" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 10</a> shows that the spectrum can be divided into three parts. The semicircle in the high-frequency region represents the diffusion resistance <i>R</i><sub>sei</sub> when Na<sup>+</sup> passes through the SEI membrane. The semicircle in the middle-frequency area represents the charge-transfer resistance <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub>. The oblique line in the low-frequency area is the solid-state diffusion process. After the first cycle, the charge transmission resistance of crab carbon is larger than that of FeS<sub>2</sub>/C and SnS<sub>2</sub>/C. It means that the SnS<sub>2</sub>/C and FeS<sub>2</sub>/C composite structures are more conducive to Na<sup>+</sup> transmission (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig10" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 10</a>a). After the 200th, the Na<sup>+</sup> transmission resistance of crab carbon is smaller than that of FeS<sub>2</sub>/C and SnS<sub>2</sub>/C (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig10" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 10</a>b). This proves that the crab carbon that contains abundant nanofiber channels is conducive to maintaining a stable structure, keeping good contact with the current collector during cycles. The FeS<sub>2</sub>/C and SnS<sub>2</sub>/C are accompanied by volume expansion during cycles, causing extrusion and pulverization, resulting in poor contact with the current collector and greater resistance.</div><figure id="fig10" data-index="10" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 10</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.2c06429/asset/images/medium/ao2c06429_0011.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.2c06429/asset/images/large/ao2c06429_0011.jpeg" id="gr10" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 10. EIS comparison of the crab carbon, SnS<sub>2</sub>/C, and SnS<sub>2</sub>/C samples at the (a) 1st and (b) 200th cycles. The EIS display of (c) crab carbon, (d) SnS<sub>2</sub>/C, and (e) SnS<sub>2</sub>/C samples, respectively.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.2c06429/asset/images/large/ao2c06429_0011.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsomega.2c06429&amp;id=fig10"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure></div><div id="sec4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i7">Conclusions</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p last">In this work, we used waste biomass crab shells as a carbon source to synthesize SnS<sub>2</sub>/C and FeS<sub>2</sub>/C composite anodes through simple hydrothermal method. Biomass carbon source has the advantages of low cost, porous fibers, and a large specific surface area, which can greatly improve the conductivity of the composite. Synthetic SnS<sub>2</sub>/C and FeS<sub>2</sub>/C samples not only have the high capacities of transition-metal sulfides but also own enhanced conductivity and transmission capabilities by combining with low-cost crab carbon. When used as the anode of sodium-ion batteries, the discharge specific capacity of the electrode is 416.5 and 393.8 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at the current density of 0.5 A g<sup>–1</sup>. This research provides an efficient route to utilize low-cost waste raw materials to construct high-specific energy sodium-ion batteries.</div></div><div class="NLM_back"><div class="article_supporting-info" id="notes1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i8">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><p class="last">The Supporting Information is available free of charge at <a href="/doi/10.1021/acsomega.2c06429?goto=supporting-info" class="ext-link">https://pubs.acs.org/doi/10.1021/acsomega.2c06429</a>.</p><ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label" id="silist"><li><p class="inline">Materials characterization, measurement of the electrochemical properties, calculation formula of capacity retention, and SEM images of crab carbon (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsomega.2c06429/suppl_file/ao2c06429_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">PDF</a>)</p></li></ul><p></p><div class="supInfoBoxOnFTP "><div class="sup-info-attachments"><ul><li><a class="suppl-anchor" href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsomega.2c06429/suppl_file/ao2c06429_si_001.pdf">ao2c06429_si_001.pdf (237.39 kb)</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="testing" data-doi="10.1021/acsomega.2c06429" data-url="https://widgets.figshare.com/static/figshare.js" id="figshare-widget" style="display: block;"></div><div class="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3" data-pb-dropzone="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3"><div data-widget-def="UX3HTMLWidget" data-widget-id="85b54935-30e9-4058-a6d2-0b40fb754a85" class="mb-4">

                        cas_cit1R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">1</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Biomass-Derived 3D Interconnected Porous Carbon-Encapsulated Nano-FeS2 for High-Performance Lithium-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Xu, Xin; Ying, Hangjun; Zhang, Shunlong; Meng, Zhen; Yan, Xufeng; Han, Wei-Qiang</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Applied Energy Materials</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2020</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">3</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">6</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">5589-5596</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">AAEMCQ</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">2574-0962</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">The application of earth-abundant materials is strategically important for industrial manuf.  Utilizing the gelatinization and metallic complexation properties of lotus rhizome starch, a FeS2/lotus rhizome starch-derived carbon (LRSC) composite was prepd. with ultrafine FeS2 particles encapsulated in hierarchically porous carbon.  The abundant interconnected macropores and micropores in the LRSC can provide diffusion channels for ions and sufficient space for the vol. change of FeS2.  Besides, a continuous interconnected carbon matrix remarkably enhances the elec. cond. of FeS2.  Therefore, the hierarchically porous carbon-stabilized FeS2 exhibits significantly improved cycling performance compared to pristine FeS2 and FeS2 combined with glucose-pyrolyzed carbon.  The electrochem. capacity of the FeS2/LRSC composite is 923.5 mA h g-1 with a capacity retention of 90.2% after 100 cycles at 0.5 A g-1, which is much higher than that of pristine FeS2 (54.6%) and FeS2/C (32.6%).  This work offers an approach for designing cost-effective and high-performance electrodes for lithium-ion batteries.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrUfMWKfJHWoLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lic3V7Z6LOYDQ"> &gt;&gt; Eng. J.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2019</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>369</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">1138</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">1149</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1016/j.cej.2019.03.096</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D369%26publication_year%3D2019%26pages%3D1138-1149%26journal%3DChem.%2BEng.%2BJ.%26author%3DL.%2BHan%26author%3DY.-l.%2BZhong%26author%3DY.%2BSu%26author%3DL.-t.%2BWang%26author%3DL.-s.%2BZhu%26author%3DX.-f.%2BFei%26author%3DY.-z.%2BDong%26author%3DG.%2BHong%26author%3DY.-t.%2BZhou%26author%3DD.%2BFang%26title%3DNanocomposites%2Bbased%2Bon%2B3D%2Bmacroporous%2Bbiomass%2Bcarbon%2Bwith%2BSnS2%2Bnanosheets%2Bhierarchical%2Bstructure%2Bfor%2Befficient%2Bremoval%2Bof%2Bhexavalent%2Bchromium%26doi%3D10.1016%252Fj.cej.2019.03.096&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsomega.2c06429&amp;doiOfLink=10.1016%2Fj.cej.2019.03.096&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit2R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">2</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Nanocomposites based on 3D macroporous biomass carbon with SnS2 nanosheets hierarchical structure for efficient removal of hexavalent chromium</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Han, Liu; Zhong, Yun-lei; Su, Yang; Wang, Li-tong; Zhu, Li-shan; Fei, Xiao-feng; Dong, You-zhen; Hong, Guo; Zhou, Ying-tang; Fang, Dong</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemical Engineering Journal (Amsterdam, Netherlands)</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2019</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">369</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">1138-1149</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">CMEJAJ</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1385-8947</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier B.V.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">In this study, a novel nanocomposite, carbon/SnS2 with 3D hierarchical macroporous (MCS), was successfully prepd. for rapid photocatalytic treatment of hexavalent chromium (Cr(VI)).  The 3D macroporous carbon (MC) can be obtained through a simple carbonization process of loofah.  Since the MCS has a large sp. surface area, it can provide numerous photocatalytic active sites and has excellent adsorption properties for the Cr(VI).  In addn., the excellent elec. cond. and unique hierarchical nanoarchitecture of macroporous biomass carbon could rapidly absorb the Cr(VI) from wastewater and provide electron transport tunnel for rapid charge sepn./transport to suppress hole electron recombination.  It is confirmed that such three dimensional photocatalytic material can quickly purify Cr(VI)-contg. wastewater under mild visible light irradn., with a efficiency of 63.2% Cr(VI) redn. within 120 mins.  Besides the high visible light-driven photocatalytic activity, MCS also exhibits excellent cycling stability.  In addn., the SEM images of MCS before and after photocatalytic removal of Cr(VI) indicate that the morphol. of composite was perfectly maintained and no peeling-off of SnS2 was obsd.  More importantly, this study demonstrated that MCS can be used for practical applications due to its stability and economic availability.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqYZNeAa34287Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lic3V7Z6LOYDQ"> &gt;&gt;                         cas_cit3R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">3</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">FeS2 nanoparticles embedded in N/S co-doped porous carbon fibers as anode for sodium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Lu, Zhenxiao; Zhai, Yanjun; Wang, Nana; Zhang, Yaohui; Xue, Pan; Guo, Meiqing; Tang, Bin; Huang, Di; Wang, Wenxian; Bai, Zhongchao; Dou, Shixue</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemical Engineering Journal (Amsterdam, Netherlands)</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2020</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">380</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">122455</span>CODEN:

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier B.V.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">FeS2 is a promising electrode material for sodium ion batteries (SIBs) because of its high theor. capacity, rich reserves, and eco-friendly nature.  In this study, N and S doped (N, S-co-doped) carbon fibers (CFs) encapsulated FeS2 nanoparticles (5-12 nm) and adherent FeS2 nanoflakes (denoted as FeS2@CF-NS), were synthesized by electrostatic spinning and subsequent thermal treatment.  In this structure, the FeS2 nanoparticles and the FeS2 nanoflakes shorten the Na+ diffusion distance; the N, S co-doping and defect-rich sites in the carbon fibers accelerate the Na+/e- transmission and buffer the vol. expansion during the Na-FeS2 conversion reaction.  These merits synergistically contribute to the notable sodium storage performance of FeS2@CF-NS.  As anode for Na-ion half batteries, the FeS2@CF-NS exhibits high capacity (637.1 mAh/g at 1 A/g after 400 cycles) and excellent rate capacity (431.1 mAh/g at 5 A/g).  Kinetic anal. confirms that this composite structure stimulates the pseudocapacitance Na+ storage mechanism and enables a capacitive contribution ratio as high as 92.7% with respect to the total capacity.  In combination with Na3V2(PO4)3-C cathode, the FeS2@CF-NS also achieves remarkably high specific capacity (561.1 mAh/g at 1 A/g after 500 cycles) and stable cyclability (338.6 mAh/g at 5 A/g after 5000 cycles) in full cells.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpAGqqRIU43QLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lic3V7Z6LOYDQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC1MXhsFKhu7fO&amp;md5=208ee1d5433c02bd79ee2e5d81460b36</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref4"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref4'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">4</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit4" data-doi="10.1016/j.jallcom.2018.06.245"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lu, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Han, T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhu, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ryu, S.                         cas_cit4R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">4</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">A facile synthesis of sandwich-structured SnS2@reduced graphene oxide with high performance for lithium-ion battery anode</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Lu, Xiaojing; Liu, Dongxu; Han, Tianli; Zhu, Mingyang; Ryu, Si Ok; Huang, Jiarui</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Alloys and Compounds</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">A sandwich-structured SnS2@reduced graphene oxide (SnS2@rGO) nanocomposite consisting of SnS2 sheets uniformly anchored on both sides of rGO sheets is presented.  The SnS2@rGO nanocomposites, prepd. through a dipping process combining with a hydrothermal method, show good electrochem. performance, including the high reversible capability of 959.2 mAh/g at 500 mA/g over 500 cycles, and superior rate performance.  A full cell consisting of a com. LiCoO2 cathode and the SnS2@rGO anode delivers the stable capacity of ∼821.3 mAh/g (anode basis) after 200 cycles at 100 mA/g.  The sandwich-structured SnS2@rGO nanocomposite accommodates vol. changes during Li+ insertion and extn. and facilitates the rapid transport of ions and electrons.  This high performance indicates that the presented SnS2@rGO nanocomposite is a promising anode candidate for practical application in Li-ion batteries.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrZxSKDIHkhzbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhPijZXCQAcrQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC1cXht1GmsbzL&amp;md5=141e182e62aa39dfbba4c8721049f4d2</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref5"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref5'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">5</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit5" data-doi="10.1016/j.jpowsour.2018.01.057"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xu, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Meng, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhu, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Han, W.-Q.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Biomass carbon composited FeS 2 as cathode materials for high-rate rechargeable lithium-ion battery</span>. <i>J.                         cas_cit5R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">5</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Biomass carbon composited FeS2 as cathode materials for high-rate rechargeable lithium-ion battery</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Xu, Xin; Meng, Zhen; Zhu, Xueling; Zhang, Shunlong; Han, Wei-Qiang</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Power Sources</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier B.V.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Pyrite FeS2 has long been used as com. primary lithium batteries at room temp.  To achieve rechargeable FeS2 battery, biomass-carbon@FeS2 composites are prepd. using green and renewable auricularia auricula as carbon source through the process of carbonization and sulfuration.  The auricularia auricula has strong swelling characteristics to absorb aq. soln. which can effectively absorb Fe ions into its body.                         cas_cit6R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">6</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Fabrication of SnS2/Mn2SnS4/Carbon Heterostructures for Sodium-Ion Batteries with High Initial Coulombic Efficiency and Cycling Stability</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Ou, Xing; Cao, Liang; Liang, Xinghui; Zheng, Fenghua; Zheng, Hong-Sheng; Yang, Xianfeng; Wang, Jeng-Han; Yang, Chenghao; Liu, Meilin</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Nano</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2019</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">13</span>
